    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_166606015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166606015" />
    <a title="J.G. Keely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely"><img alt="J.G. Keely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166606015?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.G. Keely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.G. Keely" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely">J.G. Keely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781857988833" href="/book/show/262356.The_Drowned_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland">uk-and-ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novella">novella</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166606015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6154042743693741026">The problem with writing a racially-charged tale of madness and death, lost deep in an alien and antagonistic jungle is that you're going to draw comparisons to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2569647' rel='nofollow noopener'>'Heart of Darkness'</a>, and that's not a comparison from which many novelists are going to emerge unscathed. The white men lose themselves in the brutality of the primordial past, going 'native', or even beyond native, but Ballard cannot match the furious voice or psychological insights of Conrad.<br/><br/>Ballard distinguishes himself as a competent</span>
  <span id="freeText6154042743693741026" style="display:none">The problem with writing a racially-charged tale of madness and death, lost deep in an alien and antagonistic jungle is that you're going to draw comparisons to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2569647" rel="nofollow noopener">'Heart of Darkness'</a>, and that's not a comparison from which many novelists are going to emerge unscathed. The white men lose themselves in the brutality of the primordial past, going 'native', or even beyond native, but Ballard cannot match the furious voice or psychological insights of Conrad.<br /><br />Ballard distinguishes himself as a competent and bold author, his style easily outdistancing his sci fi contemporaries. His prose is strong, his use of science thoughtful and inspired, and his setting intriguing. Of course, the very notion of a 'drowned world' is a bit suspect, as any researches into Nasa's water level software show: even with the most generous estimates, there just isn't enough water to cover it all. In a more fantastical book, like Kavan's <i>Ice</i>, we can take for granted that the setting is symbolic, not literal, but Ballard takes a more realistic approach. His use of constant, running floods alleviates some of that problem, and there still is land, made remote not just by water, but by temperature.<br /><br />Generally, as temperature rises grow more extreme, they tend to affect colder regions more than warm ones. In the Eocine, when the poles were warm year round and the ice caps entirely melted, the temperature variance from equator to pole was relatively slight--a continuous rainforest ran from pole to pole. The temperature increase had a more pronounced effect on the cold areas than the warm ones, meaning temperatures at the equator rose a small amount, while those at the poles rose a great amount.<br /><br />In the Mesozoic, a great deal of the high temperatures were the result of a combination of carbon effects and the fact that there was only a single landmass surrounded by water. Coastal regions always have less temperature variance, especially with the sorts of constant storms described in Ballard's book. These storms would also act to deflect a great deal of the sun's heat, especially as they seem to be concentrated around the equatorial regions. But these are relatively minor concerns, and Ballard's setting was strong enough to suspend disbelief, and did not delve too deeply into explanation or theory.<br /><br />His sweltering jungle world is reminiscent of Armina's Garden: the place which is too green, too full of life, so vibrant and pulsing that it grows dangerous. Everything is wet, overgrown rot, the sort of place that birthed malaria, an overabundance of frantic, competitive live for which soft man is hardly suited. His sci fi contribution to this trope is the metaphor of the primordial womb: the return to an earlier state. In this he evokes the early Twilight Zone notion of 'reversible evolution', though his depiction is less ridiculous.<br /><br />But Conrad's 'Heart of Darkness' also posits a return to an earlier psychological state, a reversion in which the 'civilized' white men grow even more brutal and depraved than they imagine the natives to be. And like Conrad, Ballard tries to trace this reversion through the psychology of his isolated, increasingly inhuman characters.<br /><br />But Ballard's psychology is not as variable, not as complex. The characters all speak in the same tones, all make the same sorts of observations, and all seem instantly to comprehend one another's minds. He seems to be trying to build a continuous, shared psychology for these latter men, to paint his reversion in absolute terms, but it is all a bit convenient, and undermines the conflicts between the characters. Their inner thoughts are explained to us, their speech prefaced by redundant adverbs.<br /><br />And this exposition is a part of his general style. His great central metaphor is also brought up often, then explained matter-of-factly. It is not an undercurrent but a tangible presence in the book, something overt which all the characters seem to understand implicitly. Yet each time we see it, we are not getting a new, contrasting view, as Conrad would give us, but the same images and observations, over and over.<br /><br />His use of figurative language is mixed. Though it sometimes hits the right mark, the constant repetition means that much of it becomes stale. some authors use such repetition to lull us, to create a poetic style where certain words, through repetition, gain more meaning over time. The author can use the word to invoke a series of related ideas and then add another layer or provide a contrasting observation.<br /><br />Through such a style, authors can create a dreamlike feel, pervaded with ideas. While Ballard can be dreamlike, he breaks up this mood by his over-explanation. The problem is, if a motif must be explained every time it is brought up, that indicates that the author has not properly established it. Instead of progressing through the idea as the story goes on, the author keeps dragging us backward.<br /><br />It felt like Ballard wanted to talk about it, but every time he returned to it, he found he had nothing new to say. Part of this might be because the book was expanded from a smaller story, but I'm not convinced it needed expansion. If anything, it could use some editing down.<br /><br />Ballard does have a literary sensibility that I respect, and I appreciated his mythological allusions, but I didn't feel like this book was a concise exploration of the concepts he brought up. We start off hard sci fi, then quickly drop into what seems like a mystical exploration of the mind, then Ballard leaves that on the back-burner for a bit of suspenseful adventure.<br /><br />There isn't really a good balance between the slow, psychological introspection and the basic adventure plot, and due to repetition, the introspection rarely elevates the story above what it might otherwise be.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6154042743693741026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2665100058" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17018779413765420944">Oh gosh I've honestly at last lost track of how many J.G. Ballard novels I've read this month. They are unique, in a way that is consistent from novel to novel but like no other author I know. I keep ping-ponging back and forth as I read these novels between thinking 'oh gosh this is so over-the-top ridiculous,' and then, kind of getting lost in the vivid technicolor imaginings of these worlds Ballard is creating and surrendering to them, and losing my sense of how I define 'ridiculous'. They ar</span>
  <span id="freeText17018779413765420944" style="display:none">Oh gosh I've honestly at last lost track of how many J.G. Ballard novels I've read this month. They are unique, in a way that is consistent from novel to novel but like no other author I know. I keep ping-ponging back and forth as I read these novels between thinking 'oh gosh this is so over-the-top ridiculous,' and then, kind of getting lost in the vivid technicolor imaginings of these worlds Ballard is creating and surrendering to them, and losing my sense of how I define 'ridiculous'. They are a little psychedelic. You need to surrender any sense of propriety or good taste to enter into them fully. <br /><br /><i>The Drowned World</i> is my favorite so far. It feels like a silly mash-up of <i>Heart of Darkness</i> with <i>The Narrative of Arthur Gordon Pym of Nantucket</i>, complete with a lascivious-albino-nihilist-sadist-petty-dictator, a deformed black henchman armed with machetes and knives, and three virtuous White Folk, who are fighting the emergence of their reptile-brain memories while at the same time drinking excellent whiskey, wearing formal evening clothes, and trying to survive the steaming jungle swamps of post-global-warming London. <br /><br />Both race and gender stereotypes abound but the story is so over-the-top that the novel feels like Ballard is poking fun at me for caring. Or maybe it feels like he is literally poking me. Or slapping me across the face. Or something.<br /><br />The novel is at its best when Ballard paints a picture of London drowning in silt--no longer a city, but instead a series of lagoons, thick with ooze, and populated by iguanas and crocodiles and giant mosquitos. <br /><br />The novel is at its hilarious, deeply readable worst whenever any humans come into the picture--here is a sample:<br /><br /><i>He started to walk towards the curtain when two of the strands parted fractionally, something moved with snake-like speed and a whirling silver blade three feet long cleft the air and spun towards his head like an immense scythe. Wincing with pain, Kerans ducked and felt the blade skim past his right shoulder, tearing a shallow three-inch weal, then impale itself with a steely shudder in the oad paneling behind him. Voice frozen in her throat, Beatrice backed wild-eyed into one of the occasional tables, knocking a chest of jewels across the floor.</i><br /><br />Darn those occasional tables! They are always in the way!</span>

<div id="review_1593817663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1593817663" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36819318-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485843897p2/36819318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1593817663?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/36819318-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1593817663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6847347182966276535">Mud. Silt. Heat. Floodwaters. And plants. Gonzo, all conquering plants spreading across a doomed earth. These are the images that stuck in my mind after reading <i> The Drowned World</i>. If you’re looking for something uplifting I advise you to look elsewhere - you’re unlikely to crack a smile at any point in this grim story. However, if you don't mind reading under a futile-struggle-against-implacable-forces cloud then Ballard's book is well worth your time - its a skilfully told story full of vivid </span>
  <span id="freeText6847347182966276535" style="display:none">Mud. Silt. Heat. Floodwaters. And plants. Gonzo, all conquering plants spreading across a doomed earth. These are the images that stuck in my mind after reading <i> The Drowned World</i>. If you’re looking for something uplifting I advise you to look elsewhere - you’re unlikely to crack a smile at any point in this grim story. However, if you don't mind reading under a futile-struggle-against-implacable-forces cloud then Ballard's book is well worth your time - its a skilfully told story full of vivid images brightly lit by a tyrannical, scorching sun.<br /><br />In our era of climate change <i> The Drowned World</i> seems to be less a novel than a prediction. In Ballard’s future the polar ice caps are long gone and the world is a baking greenhouse riven by ever expanding uninhabitable equatorial storm belts. Billions are dead and the last real human settlements cling to the poles, where temperatures are still bearable. While Ballard’s reason for this occurrence is not anthropogenic - unusual solar activity has partly stripped earth’s atmosphere away – the result is similar to those posited by worst-case scenario climate change models; a hostile planet no longer able to support human life.<br /><br />Yes, you've seen this scenario, more or less, many times before (Paolo Bacigalupi did it well with his near-submerged Bangkok in <i>The Windup Girl</i>), but Ballard goes further than your typical climate-change-ruined-my-civilisation dystopia.<br /><br /><i>The Drowned World</i> is more than an environmental apocalypse story – it’s a psychological journey, working from the premise that as our world becomes hotter and more like the sultry, reptile-friendly Triassic era human minds begin to revert back to pre-historic instincts and behaviours buried deep in our brains (Did you see that coming? I certainly didn’t!). Essentially the massive environmental changes that occur in the external world have corresponding changes in the internal emotional and mental world of human beings.<br /><br />The story focuses on Robert Kerans, a member of a research team sent to an inundated future London to investigate the changes that have occurred as the climate has shifted. From the early stages of the story, where Kerans occupies the top floors of a luxury hotel jutting from the floodwaters - its faded grandeur and sputtering aircon are a fantastic contrast with the oppressive tropics of the new London and it’s Iguana infested swamps – Ballard sets a memorable and unnerving scene. The longer Kerans and others in the expedition stay in the heat and stink of the postdiluvian city the more they change, their minds and instincts slowly warping to fit the new environmental paradigm.<br /><br /><i>The Drowned World</i> is great read, and it's hard to believe that it was written in 1962. Ballard’s book has aged well, with few of the annoying tells that often infest older sci-fi works (reel-to-reel tape systems in the year 2264, characters chain-smoking Camels in their spacecraft, that kind of thing). Ballard is a hell of a writer and he drew me right in, keeping me glued to the book right to the inevitably bleak end. I’m a winter person, fond of snow and long coats, so the ceaselessly sweltering earth of <i>The Drowned World</i> would be a special kind of hell for me, a hell that is vividly and memorably evoked.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6847347182966276535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_902348386" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/902348386" />
    <a title="Lobstergirl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl"><img alt="Lobstergirl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380256801p2/1910836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/902348386?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lobstergirl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lobstergirl" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl">Lobstergirl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Publius Clodius Pulcher</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1910836-lobstergirl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer902348386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7924597308317055514"><br/>I liked the basic premise of <i>The Drowned World</i>: in 2145, a tiny handful of hardy souls is moored in a superheated, drowned city (which turns out to be London), surrounded by deep lagoons - they have to live near the tops of buildings because everything below is flooded, silt-covered, and seaweed-smeared - just finishing up some science things before returning to the Arctic Circle where the temperature is a more comfortable 85°. I liked most of the thick description. Nice nouns and adjectives, mo</span>
  <span id="freeText7924597308317055514" style="display:none"><br />I liked the basic premise of <i>The Drowned World</i>: in 2145, a tiny handful of hardy souls is moored in a superheated, drowned city (which turns out to be London), surrounded by deep lagoons - they have to live near the tops of buildings because everything below is flooded, silt-covered, and seaweed-smeared - just finishing up some science things before returning to the Arctic Circle where the temperature is a more comfortable 85°. I liked most of the thick description. Nice nouns and adjectives, mostly. (The overheating and flooding was caused not by burning fossil fuels, but by a series of violent and prolonged solar storms which began to melt the ice caps.)<br /><br />But there was too much silliness: people are still playing records on record-players and typing on typewriters; black people are still Negroes; Beatrice Dahl spends much of the novel in a full length brocade gown (did Ballard understand what brocade is? It's thick and heavy, curtain material, and no sane person would be wearing brocade with a temperature over 95°). It was unclear how the protagonists had a constant supply of fresh water, and how other things like alcohol, suntan oil, and women's makeup never seemed to run out. Some of the characters remain sane and understand that they cannot live in a superheated London for long, but other characters are summoned by deep "neuronic" impulses encoded in their atavistic genes to revert to their Triassic era longings, when apparently all creatures pined for the sun. So the two or three crazy people flee superheated London and make their way south, via whatever boat-type apparatus they can cobble together, toward the Equator. At this point during daylight hours the temperature is in the range of 140°-150°, at which point life is not sustainable. Yet they sustain. (Where is their water coming from?)<br /><br />There was a whole long plotline where looters - basically a big bunch of Negroes led by a sinister white man who was able to command herds of giant alligators - infested the drowned city and harassed the others. I was not into this plotline at all.<br /><br />I might have generously given this 3 stars were it not for 3 appalling typos or grammatical errors; have these been in the text since 1962? One shudders. On p. 89 "ours" is spelled "our's." On p. 129 (this is so painful) Ballard uses "I" when he should be using "me." And on p. 167 we read about a "yoke" sac.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7924597308317055514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1083418403" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1083418403" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1083418403?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=blew-it-up-you-maniacs">blew-it-up-you-maniacs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas">of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=burns-like-cold-iron">burns-like-cold-iron</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1083418403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4313924530615604525">Nutshell: though global warming wins, cagey survivors succumb only to evopsychomachia. <br/><br/>Global warming is merely the Luca Brasi of a villainous sun, whose “sudden instability” “enlarged the Van Allen belts and diminished Earth’s gravitational hold upon the outer layers of the ionosphere” (33). Increased radiation dicked up the temperature, accelerated plant growth, and mutated the fuck outta everyone else (id.). The heat afflicted routine hydrologies, and now “the Middle West of the United States</span>
  <span id="freeText4313924530615604525" style="display:none">Nutshell: though global warming wins, cagey survivors succumb only to evopsychomachia. <br /><br />Global warming is merely the Luca Brasi of a villainous sun, whose “sudden instability” “enlarged the Van Allen belts and diminished Earth’s gravitational hold upon the outer layers of the ionosphere” (33). Increased radiation dicked up the temperature, accelerated plant growth, and mutated the fuck outta everyone else (id.). The heat afflicted routine hydrologies, and now “the Middle West of the United States, filled with the Mississippi as it drained the Rocky Mountains, became an enormous gulf opening into the Hudson Bay, while the Caribbean Sea was transformed into a desert of silt and salt flats. Europe became a system of giant lagoons” (34). About five million human persons remain, living mostly on the poles (35). <br /><br />So, yeah, the normal post-apocalyptic Setting, which, as we shall recall, sucks as a rule, being filled with inarticulate menace (here by reptiles and pirates and weather and whatnot). Similarly, the Story sucks in the routine post-apocalypse, consisting of the protagonist’s insignificant struggles against the purposeless menace of the sucky Setting. <br /><br />World is taken over by atavistic “intruders from the Triassic past” (21), which probably doesn’t make a whole lot of scientific sense. The atavistic life forms are apparently in some sort of Freudian or Marxist relation with the geologically appropriate ones, insofar as “the reptiles had gone down before the emergent mammals” and have an “implacable hatred one zoological class feels towards another that usurps it” (30). (Don’t look at me; I’m scratching my head, too.)<br /><br />Psychologist in the narrative develops the theory that, consistent with the “same avalanche backwards into the past” (54), survivors will “descend back into the neuronic past” (56), which strikes me as some kind of evopsych tomfoolery, the mind adopting antediluvian mannerisms to become “a traveller in a Wellsian time machine” (57). Survivors are accordingly afflicted by “ancient organic memory millions of years old” (89). <br /><br />Anyway, protagonist buggers off to be a troglodyte in accordance with Darwinian-hobbesian imperatives. Or something.<br /><br />Recommended for persons thrown up out of Earth’s magma by some vast natural cataclysm, embalmed in the gigantic intervals of time that had elapsed during their subsidence, inverted Crusoes who deliberately maroon themselves, and corpses floating out in their unraveling winding sheets in a grim rehearsal of the Day of Judgment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4313924530615604525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ubE3KO06DFDJuXwm/Z7C2plUgvKG1kVuyIlIGqDJljKDl176VmTFOG6SyEcKa8gFTwtlOkhbywmT9w6rbYpkqA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('moCuB+HPP1zNPk/eB7jfaiUMrFAlj4k0ttYSBYChvNCgpsfVWpH2NGoV+7/wTdW181NLmOsCB1PtqFS0TeJOSg==')})
  }

After losing her job when the Garmin factory shuts down, Nonie is left with major money issues—as in, she has none. Desperate, she has to turn to the one place she’s avoided working since dropping out of college--her family’s funeral home.It wasn’t that Nonie feared the dead. In fact the opposite was true. She sees them—literally, and had been seeing the dead ever since she can remember. She tried telling her parents about it when she was a child, but they’d vehemently dismissed it to imaginary friends, so she’d stopped talking about it. There were only two people who actually knew Nonie’s secret and knew she was the real deal. One was Buggy Mouton, her best friend since kindergarten, and the other was Guy Skinard, whom Nonie considered her one true love since the day she’d laid eyes on him in high school. Buggy now worked at MeeMaw’s Café off of Main in Clay Point, and Guy, as usual, remained ever at her side—the only difference being he’d died in a freak boating accident nine years ago. Still the prankster even in death, Guy loved causing chaos, especially with men Nonie occasionally dated. She could only imagine what mayhem he might stir up while mirroring her at the funeral home and mingling with the newly departed.Only hours into the first day of her new job, pandemonium engulfs the funeral home, and much to Nonie’s surprise, it has nothing to do with Guy. It’s brought on by the family they’re serving. Seventy-two–year-old Dover Fontenot, the Major of Clay Point, dies in a car accident after indulging in a little extra-curricular activity—only the activity wasn’t with his wife , Hazel. And when Dover’s mistress, Anna Mae Turner, shows up at the funeral home for the visitation hell’s gates rip open and Broussard’s Funeral Home suddenly turns into a geriatric war zone.Amidst the flying hairpieces, canes and curses, Nonie’s best friend, Buggy, decides to drop by the funeral home with news she can’t wait to share. A cable network in New Orleans wants to put together a paranormal investigation team and have them check out purported haunted locations in Louisiana. For every location with solid filming potential, each member of the team gets a five hundred dollar pay check. And Buggy knows without question that if anyone can sniff out the ‘restless’ dead, it’s Nonie.Knowing nothing about paranormal investigation, but lured by the sound of the adventure and the dollar signs circling through her head, Nonie agrees to become part of the Boo Krewe, the name Buggy has formally given the makeshift team.The lure of money may be one thing, but what Nonie doesn’t realize is that occasionally the dead, so excited that someone can actually see and hear them, decide to follow her home, into her bed—the bathroom—the shower. And adding to this new-found frustration, Nonie barely finishes her first investigation when she gets a visit from Anna Mae Turner—the mayor’s former mistress—now dead, as well, and harassingly insistent that Nonie bring her killer to light.Nonie quickly learns that the ‘gift’ she’s carried since birth is really a giant wart on the butt of life—and no amount of Compound W is going to make it       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7010931.J_G_Ballard">About J.G. Ballard</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7010931.J_G_Ballard"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1497551843p3/7010931.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7010931.J_G_Ballard">J.G. Ballard
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,610
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3039908704532391880">James Graham "J. G." Ballard (15 November 1930 – 19 April 2009) was an English novelist, short story writer, and essayist. Ballard came to be associated with the New Wave of science fiction early in his career with apocalyptic (or post-apocalyptic) novels such as <i>The Drowned World</i> (1962), <i>The Burning World</i> (1964), and <i>The Crystal World</i> (1966). In the late 1960s and early 1970s Ballard focused on a</span>
  <span id="freeText3039908704532391880" style="display:none">James Graham "J. G." Ballard (15 November 1930 – 19 April 2009) was an English novelist, short story writer, and essayist. Ballard came to be associated with the New Wave of science fiction early in his career with apocalyptic (or post-apocalyptic) novels such as <i>The Drowned World</i> (1962), <i>The Burning World</i> (1964), and <i>The Crystal World</i> (1966). In the late 1960s and early 1970s Ballard focused on an eclectic variety of short stories (or "condensed novels") such as <i>The Atrocity Exhibition</i> (1970), which drew closer comparison with the work of postmodernist writers such as William S. Burroughs. In 1973 the highly controversial novel <i>Crash</i> was published, a story about symphorophilia and car crash fetishism; the protagonist becomes sexually aroused by staging and participating in real car crashes. The story was later adapted into a film of the same name by Canadian director David Cronenberg.<br /><br />While many of Ballard's stories are thematically and narratively unusual, he is perhaps best known for his relatively conventional war novel, <i>Empire of the Sun</i> (1984), a semi-autobiographical account of a young boy's experiences in Shanghai during the Second Sino-Japanese War as it came to be occupied by the Japanese Imperial Army. Described as "The best British novel about the Second World War" by <i>The Guardian</i>, the story was adapted into a 1987 film by Steven Spielberg.<br /><br />The literary distinctiveness of Ballard's work has given rise to the adjective "Ballardian", defined by the <i>Collins English Dictionary</i> as "resembling or suggestive of the conditions described in J. G. Ballard's novels and stories, especially dystopian modernity, bleak man-made landscapes and the psychological effects of technological, social or environmental developments." The <i>Oxford Dictionary of National Biography</i> entry describes Ballard's work as being occupied with "eros, thanatos, mass media and emergent technologies".</span>
  <a data-text-id="3039908704532391880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
